                           </msup>
                           <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                           <mn>19</mn>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                              <mo>−</mo>
                           </msup>
                           <mo>↔</mo>
                           <mn>23</mn>
                           <mi>KSn</mi>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{\rm{K}}}_4{\rm{Sn}}_{23} + 19{{\rm{K}}}^ + + 19{{\rm{e}}}^ - \leftrightarrow 23{\rm{KSn}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(6)</span></div>The working mechanism of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> involving the conversion and alloying reactions upon potassiation was further exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0005">5c</a>. At first, the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> crystal was converted into Sn and K<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>. Subsequently, a cubic K<sub>4</sub>Sn<sub>23</sub> intermediate phase was formed due to the futher potassiation. At the end of potassiation, the product of KSn was formed. After complete depotassiation, Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> was partially recovered along with the intermediate of Sn. To our knowledge, it is the first evidence-based report on the reaction mechanism of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> acting as the anode of PIBs.</div>
         
         <p>To investigate the effect of morphology for K ion storage, we provide the TEM images of electrodes after 300 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). From the TEM images, the SEI layers of all the electrodes can be clearly observed. The Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 exhibits a thin and uniform SEI layer (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). The stable SEI formation to the outer surface of the superstructure. It can be seen that the surface is smooth and naked. The quality SEI layer helps maintain the cyclic stability of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48. However, small particles can be found on the surface in the SEI layer of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-24 (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26b</a>, Supporting Information), indicating an inhomogeneous SEI layer. Different from the stable and uniform SEI layer of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48, the SEI layer of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200 is uneven and broken (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26c</a>, Supporting Information). The re-formation of SEI led to the continuous decomposition of electrolyte and reducing both the capacity and cycling stability. We provide XPS to further analyze the effect of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) for K-ion storage, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information). Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27a–c</a> (Supporting Information) shows the high resolution C 1<i>s</i> spectra of a) Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48, b) Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-24, and c) Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200 after 300 cycles. It can be seen that the SEI film of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-24 and Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200 contained abundant organic components (C<span class="icomoon"></span>O and CO), demonstrated that the severe decomposition of the solvents in the electrolyte. It would cause the capacity fading. The content of organic components was significantly lower in the SEI of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48, implied that the decrease of solvation decomposition. We can distinctly observe that the electrode present different F1<i>s</i> XPS spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27d</a>-f&nbsp;(Supporting Information). The F1<i>s</i> XPS spectra could be deconvoluted to distinct peaks at 687.0 and 684.1&nbsp;eV, which were corresponding to the C<span class="icomoon"></span>F bond and K<span class="icomoon"></span>F bond, respectively. From the results of XPS, the K<span class="icomoon"></span>F content of electrode after cycles in the SEI of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 higher than that of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-24 and Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200. Since the enhanced of K<span class="icomoon"></span>F was beneficial in improving the stability of SEI which prevented the continuous depletion of electrolyte and enhanced the electrode stability for KIBs.</p>
         
         <p>To obtain fundamental insight into Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC on the electronic conductivity and structure stability, DFT calculations were conducted. The optimized model diagrams of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC were presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information). The electronic structures of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> and Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC were decided by the partial density of states (PDOS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0005">5d,e</a>). Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC possessed a much smaller band gap in comparison to Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>. This indicated that the heterostructure changed the electron distribution and contributed to the movement of the conduction band toward the Fermi level. As shown in the charge density difference plot (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0005">5f</a>), the obvious charge transformation from Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> to PC in the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC heterostructure resulted in a firm and intimate contact while improving the structure stability. Moreover, the introduction of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> in the Sn@PC heterostructure (Sn-PO<sub>4</sub>@PC) was helpful to increase the electron injection (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0005">5g</a>). It also induced a charge transfer to form positively and negatively charged surfaces, which was favorable to improving the adsorption and transfer kinetics of K<sup>+</sup>. The binding energies of K ions (Δ<i>E</i><sub>b</sub>) in Sn@PC, Sn-PO<sub>4</sub>@PC, and Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC were also calculated for comparison. Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information) presented the structure models of Sn@PC, Sn-PO<sub>4</sub>@PC and Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC during the potassiation and depotassiation states. Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC possessed the lowest binding energy (−9.75&nbsp;eV), smaller than those of Sn-PO<sub>4</sub>@PC (−3.68&nbsp;eV), and Sn@PC (−2.44&nbsp;eV). It suggested that the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC was the most energetically favorable for K adsorption (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0006">6a</a>). The optimized electronic structure and strong K binding ability for Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC accounted for its fast and stable K storage performance.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs5581-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d736a600-9ece-4937-b19f-424484eff967/advs5581-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d736a600-9ece-4937-b19f-424484eff967/advs5581-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8a25c8c8-b5a2-43de-8173-1449efb841f6/advs5581-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d736a600-9ece-4937-b19f-424484eff967/advs5581-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs5581-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202206587" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Binding energies (Δ<i>E</i><sub>b</sub>) for potassium storage of Sn@PC. b) Calculated volume expansion of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> unit cell after potassiation. c) Overall and d) internal perspective stress contours of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200. e) Cross-sectional stress contours of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles in Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200. f) Overall and g) internal perspective stress contours of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48. h) Cross-sectional stress contours of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles in Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To understand the correlation between the structural variation and mechanical properties, the volume expansion and structure stress after ion intercalation was simulated by DFT calculation and FEA analysis. Detailed information regarding the simulation parameters was provided in the Supporting Information. It was obvious that the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> had undergone a huge volume expansion (≈120%) after embedding potassium atoms (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0006">6b</a>). Therefore, the mechanical strength of the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC superstructures was further analyzed by FEA.</p>
         
         <p>Different models (Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 and Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200) were established to demonstrate the stress distribution after 120% volume expansion by FEA. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0006">6c–h</a> displayed the stress contours and the corresponding stress colormaps of different models. For Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200, the stress concentration distribution gradually increased from the inner to the outer of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0006">6c–e</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information). A significant stress region with a maximum stress value of 3.13&nbsp;GPa was produced on the surface contact area between the carbon and the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles. Compared to Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200, the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 possessed maximum stress of 3.01&nbsp;GPa. The stress concentrated area was distributed on its contact surface (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0006">6f,j,h</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29b</a>, Supporting Information), which indicated the size-dependent electro-chemomechanical behavior. Reducing the particle size of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> in Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC superstructure was favorable for releasing the stress and improving cycle stability. Based on the above analysis, the unique morphology and ultrathin particle size in the superstructure played a vital role in stress release and enhancing its lifespan.</p>
         
         <p>In view of the excellent performances of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 in half cells, it is tested in full cells by using homemade prussian blue (PB) as cathode material (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information). The full cell was tested within 0.8–3.2&nbsp;V at density of 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup><sub>anode</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a> (Supporting Information), the full cell displays a very stable cycling. After 100 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity is still 71.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention of 92.8%.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Inspired by the remarkable performance of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 electrode in PIBs, the Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance and mechanism of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 were further evaluated. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0007">7a</a>, for SIBs, the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 delivered 96.0% capacity retain after 8000 cycles, with a capacity of 202.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which exceeds those of most reported anodes for SIBs (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The CE stabilized at ≈99.69% for 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> throughout the cycles. In contrast, the cycling stabilities of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-24 and Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200 for sodium storage were inferior compared to Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a>, Supporting Information). The capacity retention of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-24 and Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-200 were only 65.8% and 19.9% after 2000 cycles. The excellent long-term reversibility of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 indicated that the unique superstructure favored the stability of the electrode during cycling. The stable superstructure can effectively resolve the critical issues of severe volume expansion and poor conductivity. The superior Na storage of the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>-based electrodes was achieved by optimizing the structure and the conductive framework. The Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms of the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> anode during charge and discharge were investigated by ex situ XRD (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0007">7b,c</a>) and ex situ HRTEM images/SAED (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0007">7d–g</a>). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0007">7b,c</a>, during the discharge process, the diffraction peaks of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> gradually weaken (1.8&nbsp;V) and completely disappeared (0.5&nbsp;V) accompanied by the emergence of Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, Sn, NaSn, Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> (0.01&nbsp;V). This indicated that the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> was fully reduced. The HRTEM analysis was performed to confirm the formation of Sn, NaSn, Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> during the full discharge state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0007">7d,e</a>). During the subsequent charging process, the diffraction peaks of Sn, NaSn, Na<sub>9</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub>15</sub>Sn<sub>4</sub> gradually weaken, whereas the phases of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> were again found (3.0&nbsp;V). HRTEM and SAED images at full charge state indicated the high reversibility of the Na storage reaction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0007">7f,g</a>). However, the peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> and Sn did not completely disappear and hence, proved that the capacity was attenuated at the beginning. The ex situ characterization outcome showed that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> was different from K<sup>+</sup>. In addition, the conversion reaction mechanism was comparatively complex in Na-ion systems. The electrochemical reactions upon cycling were shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs5581-fig-0007">7h</a> and could be described by Equations&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#advs5581-disp-0007" title="Link to equation">7-8</a>, and&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#advs5581-disp-0009" title="Link to equation">9</a>.

            <div class="inline-equation" id="advs5581-disp-0007"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a131777a-c395-4f44-9fe0-a795c4e75e24/advs5581-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs5581:advs5581-math-0007" display="block" location="graphic/advs5581-math-0007.png">

         </div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="advs5581-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1c06ca43-d286-48ce-a271-a6a1b4310e88/advs5581-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1c06ca43-d286-48ce-a271-a6a1b4310e88/advs5581-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8cdc66cd-8b88-4886-a6f4-b4267176ac9f/advs5581-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1c06ca43-d286-48ce-a271-a6a1b4310e88/advs5581-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs5581-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202206587" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Long-term cycling performance of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 at a current density of 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in SIBs. b,c) Ex situ XRD patterns of the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 electrode at different charge/discharge states of SIBs. d) TEM image and e) SAED pattern of the fully discharged Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 electrode in SIBs. f) TEM image and g) SAED pattern of the fully charged Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 electrode in SIBs. h) The schematic illustration of the reaction mechanism for Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC of sodium insertion/ extraction.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>We assembled full cells using Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 as anode and homemade sodium Prussian blue analog as the cathode. The full cell was tested within 0.8–3.2&nbsp;V at a current density of 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup><sub>anode</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a> (Supporting Information), the full cell exhibited a good stability. After 80 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity was still 117.4&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup><sub>anode</sub>. The good stability was benefitting from the well-designed superstructure of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48, and the structure was not cracked and there was no excessive defect induced during the repeated cycling.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs5581-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs5581-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Inspired by the controllable CPA growing process of Sn-based phosphate MOFs, we had constructed an Sn-MOF superstructure derived Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanocrystal confined in the interlinked carbon framework. Owing to the synergistic effect between the unique superstructure and the excellent intrinsic activity of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>, the Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC-48 presented high capacity and outstanding cycle stability for PIBs and SIBs (324&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles). Even after 10000 cycles at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> the capacity retention remained at ≈90.1% for PIBs and a reversible capacity of 202.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with 96.0% capacity retain after 8000 cycles for SIBs. It is one of the best performances among the anodes for K<sup>+</sup>/ Na<sup>+</sup> storage reported so far. Systematic physicochemical/structural analysis combined with DFT calculation and FEA revealed that the intrinsic property of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC and its unique superstructure cooperated to boost the K<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics and performance. The reason was attributed to the promoted electronic conductivity, enhanced K binding ability and improved structure stabilization. The maximum stress significantly decreased with the size reduction of Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles in Sn<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>@PC superstructure, which significantly reduced the risk of surface cracks and particle pulverization during potassiation/ sodiation. The new findings could open the door for exploring more innovations of microstructures and functionalities for applications in energy storage and other fields by control the crystallization process in MOFs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
